1. What Is CRO
All right, welcome to the long-awaited conversion rate optimization program course. First off, I
want to start this by saying I am very aware that I look homeless right now. I haven't had a haircut
in over a month.
I was supposed to have a haircut last week and I completely missed it because I have been living
in a blur. But starting things off the right way here in typical Spencer fashion, not even on point.
What I'm going to be going through today is conversion rate optimization and what it actually is.
So that way, if you don't know anything about it, or maybe you've actually heard about it, or you're
coming in as a CRO expert, I'm going to be walking you through from my perspective and from
my point of view, what CRO actually is. I'm not an absolute guru on this. I have not been doing
CRO for the last 10, 15, 20 years.
I understand that there's people in this industry that have been doing it for a very long time. What
I can tell you is that I've tried a lot of things in the last three years, and I've had some things work.
I've had some things not work.
And I've had a lot of really smart people tell me what they do and help me better understand what
CRO is in general. So what I want to do before we get into all the good stuff, because at the end
of the day, we're going to get into a lot of the good stuff. We're going to get into all the tactical
things and the tools and all of that amazing stuff.
What I actually want to just focus on here today is more around what CRO is. Before I actually
explain what conversion rate optimization, or if you hear me say CRO, that's what CRO stands
for, conversion rate optimization. I want you to first understand what CRO is not.
For me, sometimes the best way to understand something is just to know what it is. There's a lot
of, again, specific views on CRO. This is just my view.
It's not the right way. It's not the wrong way. But for me, it's just how I view it.
And so the first thing that CRO is not, if you ask me, is about increasing your Shopify store

FILE: 13. Intelligems - The Importance Of QA.txt

13. Intelligems - The Importance Of QA
Alright, now the final thing I want to mention before you actually launch your test is to please
make sure that you QA your pages extremely well. You'll notice that before I press start,
IntelliGems is actually going to tell you to make sure that you have QA'd the test, okay? And the
reason why that is so important to do is because you wouldn't believe how many times things do
not work simply because you have messed something up. So I'm going to give you some
examples of where we forgot to QA or we thought we QA'd well and it actually led to a bunch of
issues.
So the first one here that I can think of is actually going back to this one over here. And so if we
actually take a look at these generic reviews here, so you can see this is V2 and then this is V1.
You can see we had a split test idea to basically have generic reviews on products, okay? And so
this client, or sorry, products that didn't have any reviews, okay? And so this didn't really hit
StatSig data, but as you can see, it was on track to lose this client a lot of money.
And so what ended up happening and what the idea of this test was, and I'll just show this case
study as an example so you understand the importance of QA and it was kind of flawed thinking
by our team, if you ask me. But basically, as you can see here, there are products that have
reviews for this client in particular. And then of course there are products that do not have
reviews, okay? And if you don't have a multi-SKU store, I don't care, don't skip this video, keep
watching.
And as you can see, there are reviews of this product in particular and of people talking about this
product. Whereas this one here has zero reviews. It's a brand new product.
It doesn't have anything, right? And so what we ended up doing and what our CR team thought
would be a good idea is to add this section to all of the products, right? We'll be like, hey, let's just
add this entire section and then let's just add all of the site-wide reviews. Because if we actually
go and take a look at this website or the full stack of reviews like this here, this was actually the
review card that we were using. And in practice, it sounded like a great idea.
And so when it ended up happening is when we reviewed this test, we were like, I can't believe
this didn't convert. And now we might be thinking like, oh, you know what? It probably didn't
convert because maybe people don't care about these reviews or whatever it is, but I knew
something was wrong. And so what happened? Well, I went deeper.
I QA'd it. And I realized that what our developers did was that they actually overrode it for
anything. So even if the product had reviews, we were replacing the product-specific reviews with
just general reviews.
And that is obviously going to hurt conversions because people don't want to just see general
reviews. They want to see reviews for their product specifically. And so what ended up

happening, as you can see, it's not showing on this page yet.
If we go back to that previous page I was on, let's see if I can get back there. This one here, you
can see it's actually showing this, right? And it has this empowered woman thing, which looks
great, right? But the issue was, is that this was replacing the reviews that we had for products
specifically. And so when we actually realized that, we're like, oh, that's probably why the profit
and revenue was going down by $60,000 a month, okay? And so what did we do? Well, we
launched Generic Imagery Reviews V2, but this time we actually did it right.
We took the time to QA, we went through, we double-checked, we made sure that there were
reviews for their products specifically. And on the brands that did not have, or the products that
did not have reviews yet, again, we had some custom development help with this, we then would
show this section. And long behold, we then added $30,000 a month in profit, pretty statistically
significant.
We had over 800 orders with 15,000 people. And now we have a winning split test, okay? And we
could have just as easily not had a winning split test because we were lazy on QA and we didn't
think through that we needed to add a condition that said, don't overwrite the reviews that are
already on the page if it has reviews. And so we made that one small change.
It was one little piece of code, and that's the difference between making $30,000 per month or
losing $60,000 per month, okay? So it is so important to make sure that you QA your work
because if you do not QA and you do not do what I talked about in the program around going
through, checking your page for bugs, checking on different browsers, checking on your phone,
checking here, checking there, loading it up in an incognito browser. So for example, one of my
favorite things to do here, let me just delete this out of here, is actually go and double check the
parameters. So for example, this split test here, I think it's running in multiple countries.

15. Intelligems - Understanding Powered Stat Sig
All right. So yeah, basically to try to help you understand a little bit more about statistical
significance in general, and like how this works, basically, if we make the decision too early, we
will more than likely push the wrong variant line. Now, in this instance, it's kind of like a 50-50
shot, right? We're testing A versus B, and right now it's saying like, this has a huge opportunity to
increase profit and revenue, and we might freak out and be like, this is exactly what we need to
do.
But the issue is, is that we don't have any orders, right? Like we have, you know, 29 orders,
which is nothing, because when we're looking at just like even what IntelliGems recommends,
they recommend a minimum of like 300 orders per group before even analyzing the results.
Again, it says like, this is not just a rule of thumb, and the right number for every brand is
different, but like, this is just at least 300 orders per group, okay? And the reason that is, is
because of the fact that here's a great example of it, just to show one that actually got StatSig
and is fully powered, where if we take a look, and I actually punched this into one of our
calculators here, you can see here what we've just done as an example is we've put in like the
571 profit per visit versus the 498 profit per visit. And when we're using this calculator, again, this
is something that can be done with the actual just total conversions or orders themselves, or if
we're tracking off a profit and revenue, that's typically just how we function is we just leave the
decimal out, and then there we go.
That gives us a rough idea, because instead of here, we were actually testing for and showing a
product that had a higher average value. At the end of the day, what I'm trying to get at here is
this is a test that won and was statistically powered, okay? And so this is our A-B test guide that
we use, it says like, is your test actually significant? Meaning that, you know, no matter what the
StatSig calculator says inside of IntelliGems, what we care about is this is actually powered. And
this is a big thing that we need to make sure that there's enough power for this to actually be true,
because for example, if we take a look at the trend chart for this, you can see that the way that
this started here is that it was kind of inconsistent for the first little while.
You can see, if you look at V2 over here, it started very, very, very, very poorly. And then at one
point, it was almost the top performing page in regards to driving revenue. And so if we even just
take a look at this from, this is March 8th, I think the test actually started, it was February 26th.
So by March 8th, if we had said, okay, well, these have reached 100 orders, and it seems like this
one here, clearly, if we're even looking at conversion rate, has a 2.36% conversion rate,
obviously, V3 is better. And then we were just to push that live, that would have been a mistake.
Because if we actually go back to the original one, you can see this was actually one of the worst
performing ones out of the test after we actually had enough data.
And so that is why if we push things live too early, it's going, it could be a very costly mistake,

because we were just very impatient in this process, and we wanted to get things live. But in the
end, if we actually wait things out and get enough data, you could see we would be dramatically
hurting ourselves, not only with our conversion rate, but overall profit as well, we'd be making less
profit. And so then, for example, that was obviously March 8th here that I checked this.
Then if we fast forward to March 22nd, you could see we were actually going down in conversion
rate. And there really wasn't any change at all. And if we look at this, this was almost coming
across as like a insignificant test.
And now as you can see here, we're at about 200 orders per variant. And now we're looking at
this, well, this is insignificant. So I guess, for example, there's nothing out of this test.
And we could have easily just canceled this test and been like, well, it didn't work. It was
insignificant. But then again, if we go back to where we actually let it finish testing to get above
300 orders, you can see not all of them at 300, but the two that were testing here do have 300.

see 400 or 500 orders and see what happens next.
Because as you can see, every hundred orders here, we went from this being a 10% increase in
conversion rate to there being a 3% decrease in conversion rate. And now the final test showing

how much it changes.
And we ended the test on April 2nd. So from, you know, March 8th over here to March 22nd to

So that is like the most important reason why we need to let things test, because we have literally
seen tests like this come out this hot, even at a hundred orders. And we're like, oh my gosh, we

And then at the end of the day, it's like, it's actually not, it's not true. It's not powered by anything.
Again, it's the ideal of flipping a coin.

you use a specific URL, which I'm actually going to give you, it won't actually show the discount
code until they get all the way to checkout or until they actually apply it at checkout.

the user to do more, it's not really great for conversion. So that's why when it comes to manual

FILE: 2. Types Of CRO.txt
======================================================================

2. Types Of CRO
Alright, now in this next module, I'm going to be walking you through the three main types of CRO
that I have come across, and kind of explain the pros and the cons of each and essentially, like
our journey through the CRO process, that'll be better understand why we use the CRO style that
we do now, and kind of just walk you through the different options that you have when it comes to
actually improving, again, the customer journey in order to scale your business further. So the
first strategy here is, I think, where most people start, because it's definitely where we started.
And to be honest, I call it the send it strategy, because that's really what you're doing.
If you've never seen this video, it's an incredible video that is a Canadian in his truest form of just
absolutely sending it. As you can see there, a lot of times you crash and burn. Okay.
And so what the send it strategy is, is it essentially is when let me just go ahead and update my
camera here. So the Senate strategy is when you don't test anything, and you essentially just
execute, okay, you go with what you believe is best, and you just hope that it works better. Now,
don't get me wrong, there are times when it does work better.
But with that said, majority of the times you have no metric that tells you that the changes that
you made are actually working. And so the reason why this is really important is because a lot of
the times when you make changes on your website, or you update something or move to a new
theme or whatever it is, you're doing it with the intention that it's going to help your conversion
rate. And logically, a lot of the times, like say, for example, just doing something like adding
reviews is a great way or adding FAQs is a great way to, again, improve the customer journey to
scale your business further.
With that said, what happens a lot of the times, and I can say this from experience is your actual
execution of it is more times than not the problem as to why things don't work. To give you an
example, we recently with a client decided that we wanted to add evergreen reviews all the way
across their site, because they only had a bunch of different SKUs, and they only had reviews on
their top selling products. But for anyone else that was maybe looking at these other products, we
wanted to make sure that they saw that there was a lot of customers that had shopped.
And in the process of doing that, our CRO team had decided that we added some kind of extra
code that showed it on top of it. And then basically what ended up happening is it ended up
actually overruling the standard reviews that we had. And so now when someone was trying to
look at a review for a specific or the reviews for our, for example, our best selling products that
actually had reviews, it was just a bunch of different products that actually wasn't that product,
which then when we split tested it, which I'm going to get to as another strategy in a second, we'll
bring this back, it ended up actually hurting conversion rate very badly.
And I was like, there's no way that something that makes logical sense should be hurting

conversion rate. And we realized that it was actually just an execution issue. And so a lot of the
times when you're following the send it strategy, it's not that your strategy is flawed, it's that the
execution is flawed.

them to buy. But a lot of the times, that's just the first click. Clicks two, three, four, five, six, 10, 20
happen on your website.
So you need to make sure that that is congruent with what you're pulling them in the type of
person that you're attracting. From there, how do you test this stuff? Again, very, very simple.
What we do is we will just duplicate the ad that we make and or that we made the page for,
because again, every landing page that you make, you should have the ad right there.
What am I building this for? No landing page should just be built without, like if you hire a CRO
agency or a landing page agency and they don't ask you for your ads, like just ask for a refund
and run. So duplicate the ad that you make, put the new URL in and you can either launch it in
the same campaign. I do the exact same thing as Nick.
I just have one campaign, that's it. But what we've recently started doing, because we have
clients and they wanna see us spending on the landing pages and stuff, is we just create a
separate LP testing campaign, exact same structure, and we set it to seven day click attribution.
And the reason why we do that is because we don't want any view through attribution when we're
testing landing pages.
We wanna make sure that people are actually clicking the ad and going and purchasing. But long
story short, we're going to then test it and we're gonna create two custom metrics, which if you
haven't created these, go and make them super easy. AOV and CVR, AOV is purchase
conversion value divided by purchases.
Conversion rate is purchases divided by landing page views. And we're gonna analyze against

the control how much our conversion rate goes up and how much our AOV goes up or down,
whatever it is, okay? And then from there, it's very simple. If it performs, scale it, that's it.
Two days in, three days in, just scale it, right? Like, you know, when you're split testing, you have
to wait two weeks, whatever it is. This is just, hey, it's ripping, let's scale, right? Two to three days,
you can start scaling. And then from there, if it's really crushing, drip it into your champions or
your winner's ad set or into your main campaign, and that's it.
If it fails, analyze why, just like you would do with an ad that fails and start building the next
iteration, okay? Another really good thing to do is if you make a landing page, like we did for that
client around gut health, continue to make ads for that page as well, right? If you find a new
winning ad or a new winning page, make ads for it. It's probably something that you could always

If you guys have ever seen this before, basically what return on ad spend is, is conversion rate
times AOV times CTR divided by CPM. A lot of you guys are right here. You're just hammering
CTR.
You're trying to get better ads, better ads, better ads, and ads can also help conversion rate as

to explode your return on ad spend, which allows you to scale further. Increasing conversion rate
and AOV helps you, again, increase profit and revenue, so that's why you should probably spend
more time optimizing them, right? Final thing I'll say, and I promise this is actually his final thing,

US or Canada or big five.

[Felix Preis](https://www.skool.com/@felix-preis-3587?g=origins)

TL;DR

First day after launch, $82 ad spend, I send my traffic straight from landing to checkout, so no cart, 20 checkouts initiated, would be almost breakeven at 2 sales, profitable with 3 sales, but got 0... What to do?

So I launched my store yesterday, 10 creatives split into 2 ad sets in 1 campaign, decent first day stats from FB perspective ($82 spent, 200 link clicks, 6.91% CTR). So far so good.

However, my landing page converted those 200 link clicks into 20 ATC's, but since I don't have a cart and send directly to checkout, it's effectively 20 checkouts initiated. The problem is that out of those 20 people, not 1 completed the purchase. What is going on here?

1. I offer 3 quantity options with increasing discount + free shipping for the 2 larger quantity options. I make it clear that you get free shipping with the 2nd and 3rd one, but I don't explicitly say you have to pay shipping for the lowest tier. (See attached screenshot) Might that be the reason? That people leave after seeing the shipping charge in the checkout? Is there a way for me to see in Shopify which quantities people added to their cart? I use Kaching for the bundle.

2\. Since I don't have any purchases yet, I can't optimize for purchase conversion events yet inside my ad account, so I had to set it up for checkouts initiated. Might that be the reason? How do I solve it then?

Thanks for the help!

3

FILE: 3. Key Metrics For CRO.txt
======================================================================

3. Key Metrics For CRO
Alright, now in this section, I'll be talking about some CRO metrics that you need to know in order
to kind of understand, I think, moving forward through the program, things that are going to come
up. And also just like general CRO, some more tactical CRO stuff for the CRO that we're
teaching, it's actually going to be based inside of the ad account, but we'll get more to that. At the
end of the day, the terms are the same, and I want to make sure that you understand these
things.
And then on top of that, I also want to go through and help you better understand just where
you're at right now, and some custom metrics that you can use to figure out kind of the overall
health of your Shopify store, ad account, all that stuff, okay? So the first thing here, just a few
terms to define them, like core metrics to know, CR or CVR means conversion rate, okay? Pretty
straightforward. AOV means average order value, hopefully you know that, if you don't, there you
go. And RPS is called revenue per session, so this is basically a function of conversion rate and
average order value.
And we'll touch more on that as well when we get to some more of the old school split testing
stuff. By the end of the day, these are three metrics that you definitely just want to know, or three
definitions that you're going to want to know. Now with that said, the next thing that's important for
you guys to know is something called a Shopify score.
Now a Shopify score is basically your average order value times your conversion rate, okay? So
for Mac, I always just use the search button here. If you have a 2.2% conversion rate, okay, and
let's just say you have a $100 average order value, then what you'll do is you will times your 2.2
conversion rate by 100, and this will give you what is called your Shopify score, or at least that's
what we call it. You can also call it a conversion score, it doesn't really matter.
This is the metric that we use to basically understand where the store is at. So, for example, if
you are 0 to 100, so say you have a 1% conversion rate and a $50 average order value, your
Shopify score would be 50, and what that tells us is that the product is not really communicating
value, or you have horrible traffic, okay? It's either one of the two, either you're just filled up with a
bunch of bot traffic or you're sending link clicks, I don't really know what you're doing, or, for
example, you just have your products priced terribly, and say, for example, you have no brand
and nobody knows who you are, and for whatever reason, people just aren't buying and you're
sending a lot of ad traffic, and at the end of the day, you're just not getting conversions, okay? So,
that is 0 to 100. Now, 100 to 250, this is where you want to continue to focus on optimizing your
store, optimizing your pages, okay? Because if you're in the 100 to 250 range, that typically

FILE: 5. REMINDER Identify The Angle Of Your Landing Page.txt

5. REMINDER Identify The Angle Of Your Landing Page
Alright, now something that's super important before you actually decide, again, what page you're
going to build is you need to understand what ad is working well in the account. And if you don't
have ads that are working well in the account, like I mentioned the previous video, then you
obviously need to find that first, or you can work on building pages that are more congruent with
those ads. But the main thing here is that we need to better understand what we're actually
building around.
And again, in this scenario, I've already chosen it, but I just want to make it crystal clear that
before you go in and you actually build a landing page, you need to understand what the actual
angle is that you're going into to build a successful landing page. And so before we go into
anything else, I know that this is the angle that I'm going after. And I know that I've done research
around this, and I'm going to continue to do research around this.
And then the offer and everything else that we create will be around, ideally, this angle. Because
again, we want things to be congruent. We want our ads that are already producing to be more
optimal.
And the way that we do that is by building a page that is congruent with it. Now, you can also
build evergreen landing pages. You totally can.
But the goal of PageDeck and what allows us to move so fast is that we have specific pages and
specific offers for our ads that are actually communicating with one another and are highly
effective because somebody's clicking that ad for a specific reason. And then they go to a page
which talks more about that specific reason rather than the generic stuff that's happening. So just
want to stress that before you get in and start building in through PageDeck that you actually go
through and you dive deeper on the specific angle that we're building the page around.

FILE: DR MARKETING PLAYBOOK FOR PRODUCT-AWARE AUDIENCES.txt
======================================================================

▄▄▄

DR MARKETING
PLAYBOOK

FOR PRODUCT-AWARE
AUDIENCES
Meta Ads • Google Search • Google Demand Gen
Third-Party Presell Funnels • PDP Optimization • Cross-Channel Strategy

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

SECTION 1: THE CORE PROBLEM
There are two fundamentally different selling approaches for DTC products online. Most brands
confuse them, use the wrong one for the wrong audience, and kill their conversion rate before
anyone even sees the offer.
This playbook breaks down exactly how to sell to product-aware audiences across every paid
channel—and where most brands mess it up.

The #1 Mistake
THE MISTAKE MOST BRANDS MAKE:
They build problem-aware landing pages for product-aware audiences.
Your customer already knows the category exists. They’ve seen the product, maybe owned one.
When you spend 60% of your page educating them on the problem, you’re talking to them like
they’ve never heard of it.
That’s why a generic PDP often beats a “perfect” long-form LP.

What Product-Aware Buyers Actually Need
These people aren’t asking “what is this?”—they’re asking “why THIS one?” Your entire funnel
should answer that single question.
What They Need

Why

Why yours over competitors

Clear differentiation in 5 seconds

A compelling offer

Bundle, guarantee, bonus—makes buying HERE obvious

Social proof

Real testimonials that confirm legitimacy

Brand trust signals

Clean design, professional photos, real packaging

Fast path to purchase

Don’t bury the buy button

The 3 Conversion Killers
1.​ Price Point — Under $50, people don’t need a thesis to buy. The decision cost is low.
Match your page depth to the price.
2.​ Product Complexity — If your product explains itself visually (a pillow, a bag, a gadget),
you don’t need 2,000 words about the mechanism. One good image does it.

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

3.​ Traffic Temperature — Cold traffic from scroll-stopping ads gave you 2 seconds of
attention. Asking them to read a novel before showing a CTA = bounce.

IMPORTANT:
If your broad PDP is outperforming your long-form LP, it’s almost always an awareness level
mismatch—not a page structure problem. Diagnose the awareness level before you optimize
anything else.

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

SECTION 2: THE HALO EFFECT
At the product-aware stage, your audience is comparing you to real brands. They’ve seen the
established players. You can’t run a premium angle and land people on a page that looks like a
2019 dropship store.

Trust Signals: What Kills vs. What Builds
✘ KILLS TRUST
AliExpress product screenshots
Inconsistent fonts and colors
No real packaging shots
Generic stock lifestyle images
Cluttered layout, no whitespace

✔ BUILDS TRUST
Professional product photography
Consistent color palette & typography
Real packaging and unboxing shots
Authentic lifestyle imagery
Clean, branded, spacious layout

THE TRUST EQUATION SHIFT:
Problem-aware buyers purchase because you described their pain perfectly.
Product-aware buyers purchase because everything signals “this is the real deal.”
Different awareness level = different trust triggers.

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

SECTION 3: META ADS — THIRD-PARTY PRESELL FUNNEL
Why Third-Party Pages Win on Meta
Don’t run ads from your brand page. Create a third-party page that looks like a publisher,
reviewer, or community. This makes the ad feel like a real person sharing a discovery, not a
brand pushing product.

Types of Third-Party Pages
Page Type

Example

Best For

Niche Magazine

“Travel Essentials Weekly”

Editorial authority

Community Page

“Frequent Flyers Club”

Peer recommendation feel

Blog / Review Site

“Smart Travel Gear”

Independent reviewer trust

Listicle Site

“Top 10 Travel Finds”

Comparison shopping format

The Ad Creative — Native But Product-Oriented
Ugly/native ads still work but the copy angle shifts completely. You’re not leading with problem
agitation anymore. You’re leading with product authority and comparison.

Ad Copy Angles That Work
EXAMPLES:
“5 reasons this became the #1 travel pillow of 2026”
“Why frequent flyers are switching to this pillow”
“I tested 12 travel pillows—this one won and it’s not close”
“I travel 30+ weeks a year. I spent $1,372 testing 10 pillows. Here’s the winner.”

Why Specificity Wins
Element

Why It Works

Specific numbers (“$1,372 on 10
pillows”)

Builds trust—concrete > vague

Authority frame (“I did the research”)

Positions you as the expert who saved them time

Curiosity gap (“Which one won?”)

Drives the click without giving away the answer

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

IMPORTANT:
Let the image do the problem agitation. Someone sleeping in a terrible neck position on a
plane—everyone’s been there. No need to explain pain in copy when the image IS the problem.

The Presell Page — What Most People Get Wrong
COMMON MISTAKE:
Most people turn the presell into another problem-aware education piece.
800 words about neck pain and sleep science. WRONG.
The presell should be almost entirely product comparison.
The presell page replaces their comparison shopping process and controls the narrative. The
frame is “we already did the homework”—not “did you know this problem exists.”

Presell Content Structure
4.​ Open with authority — who you are, why you tested, how many you compared
5.​ Show the field — here’s what’s out there, 3–5 options in the category
6.​ Eliminate competitors — where each falls short on materials, design, durability
7.​ Crown the winner — why this specific product wins on the criteria that matter
8.​ CTA to branded store — link to your PDP where the offer is waiting

The Full Meta Funnel
Step

Channel

What Happens

Lands On

1

Meta Ad

Ugly/native ad from 3rd party page.
Product-oriented copy.

Presell article

2

Presell Page

Listicle comparing 3–5 options. Yours wins.
Builds conviction.

Branded PDP

3

Branded PDP

Clean, fast, offer-focused. Strong
bundle/guarantee.

Checkout

IMPORTANT:
The extra step in the funnel seems like it would kill conversion, but it actually increases buyer
quality and AOV. By the time they hit your store they’re pre-sold, not just curious.

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

SECTION 4: GOOGLE SEARCH — HIGH-INTENT BUYERS
Google Search is the most product-aware channel that exists. These people are actively typing
in what they want to buy. The DR approach here is different from Meta because intent is already
sky-high.

Keyword Strategy by Awareness Level
Keyword Type

Examples

Landing Destination

Brand

“best travel pillow”, “[your brand]
pillow”

Branded PDP with strong offer

Comparison

“[brand] vs [competitor]”, “best
pillow for long flights”

Presell comparison page or listicle

Category

“travel pillow”, “neck pillow for
planes”

PDP or comparison listicle

Review

“[your brand] review”, “is [brand]
worth it”

Third-party review page / presell

The DR Hack for Google Search
Run search ads to your own third-party presell pages for comparison and review keywords.
Instead of sending “best travel pillow” traffic to your PDP where you’re just one option, send it to
a listicle YOU control where you’re the #1 pick.
•​

Comparison keywords → Your listicle: “We tested 10 travel pillows. Here’s our
ranking.”

•​

Review keywords → Your review page: Independent-looking deep dive on your product

•​

Category keywords → Split test PDP vs presell. Often the presell wins for generic
terms

IMPORTANT:
Build the presell pages under a separate domain (not your brand domain). This makes the
editorial angle believable and avoids the “brand reviewing itself” credibility problem.

Google Search Ad Copy for Product-Aware

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

Your ad copy should lead with differentiation and offer, not problem agitation. These people
know what they want—show them why yours wins.
EXAMPLES:
Lead with proof — “Rated #1 by 10,000+ travelers”
Lead with offer — “Free carrying case + 90-day flight guarantee”
Lead with comparison — “Outperforms [category leader] at half the price”
Use sitelinks for objections — Reviews, Guarantee, How It Works, Compare

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

SECTION 5: GOOGLE DEMAND GEN — VISUAL DISCOVERY
AT SCALE
Google Demand Gen is Google’s answer to Meta’s prospecting machine. It serves visual,
scroll-stopping creative across YouTube, YouTube Shorts, Discover feed, and Gmail. For
product-aware audiences, it’s a massive opportunity most DTC brands are ignoring.

Why Demand Gen Works for Product-Aware
Factor

Why It Matters

Visual-first format

Your product sells itself in images and short video—Demand
Gen is built for this

Discovery feed placement

Catches people browsing content, similar to Meta’s feed but
with Google’s intent signals

YouTube Shorts

Short-form native video performs identically to Meta
Reels—same creative works

Gmail placements

Lands in the Promotions tab—feels like an email, not an ad

Audience signals

Layer Google’s search data on top of visual prospecting—intent
+ interruption combined

Creative Strategy for Demand Gen
The biggest mistake is running brand-polished creative that looks like a display ad. Demand
Gen rewards native-feeling content—the same approach that wins on Meta.

What Works
•​

Product-in-use lifestyle images — not white-background catalog shots. Show the
product in real context.

•​

Short-form UGC video (6–15 sec) — same ugly/native style from Meta. Repurpose
your best performers.

•​

Listicle-style carousel — multiple images that tell a comparison story: “Why 50,000
travelers chose this one.”

•​

Before/after or side-by-side — visual proof that your product is the obvious pick vs.
alternatives.

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

What Doesn’t Work
•​

Polished brand video — gets skipped instantly. Looks like an ad, performs like an ad.

•​

Problem-aware hooks — same mistake as Meta. They don’t need education—they
need a reason to choose YOU.

•​

Generic product shots — white background hero images get ignored in a feed full of
content.

The DR Layer on Demand Gen
Most brands treat Demand Gen as a brand awareness play. Here’s how DR marketers use it
differently:
•​

Run presell creative — Same third-party listicle angle from Meta. “We tested 10
pillows” works on YouTube Shorts and Discover.

•​

Use search term custom segments — Target people who Googled “best travel pillow”
but didn’t click your Search ad. Now you’re hitting them visually.

•​

Cross-pollinate with Meta — Your winning Meta creative is your starting point. Same
ugly ads, same product-oriented copy.

•​

Land on the presell page — NOT your PDP. Same funnel as Meta: ad → presell →
PDP.

IMPORTANT:
Demand Gen is the channel where Meta creative meets Google intent data. If you’re scaling on
Meta and not testing Demand Gen, you’re leaving money on the table. Same creative, different
audience signals, incremental reach.

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

SECTION 6: THE PRODUCT-AWARE PDP
Whether traffic comes from Meta presell, Google Search, or Demand Gen, they all land on your
PDP. This page has one job: convert a convinced visitor into a buyer. Speed and clarity beat
education.

The Hybrid PDP Structure
Top 20% functions like a fast PDP. Bottom 80% provides reassurance for scrollers. This
captures impulse buyers AND researchers.

Above the Fold (The Fast Lane)
9.​ Hero product image — professional, aspirational, shows the product in use
10.​One-line core benefit — not a paragraph, one punchy line
11.​Price + offer callout — bundle value, savings, or free bonus visible immediately
12.​Star rating + review count — social proof at a glance
13.​Add to cart button — prominent, above the fold, no scrolling required

Below the Fold (The Reassurance Lane)
14.​Quick visual comparison — yours vs alternatives, side-by-side images not paragraphs
15.​2–3 best testimonials — specific results, not generic “love it!” quotes
16.​Guarantee callout — risk reversal with a specific, memorable guarantee
17.​Second CTA — repeat the offer and buy button

What to Cut
KILL THESE SECTIONS:
The mechanism explanation—product-aware buyers don’t need it.
The problem agitation section—they already know the problem.
The “lost vacation day calculator”—nobody on cold traffic is doing math. Save it for retargeting
email.
The expert endorsement wall—one credibility badge is enough.

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

IMPORTANT:
Sticky add-to-cart is non-negotiable at any price point. If you change nothing else, add this
today. It’s the easiest conversion win you’ll get this week.

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

SECTION 7: FULL CHANNEL PLAYBOOK
Here’s how all channels work together in a product-aware DR system. Each channel has a
different entry point but they all feed the same conversion engine.

Channel-by-Channel Breakdown
Channel

Ad Format

Landing Destination

Key Principle

Meta Ads

Native/ugly from 3rd
party page

Presell → PDP

Pre-sell through
comparison

comparison

keyword)

expert quotes

The Golden Rule

PRESELL = WHY THIS ONE
PDP = BUY IT NOW
Clean separation. The presell handles conviction. The PDP handles conversion. Never mix the
two. When you try to do both on one page, you get a long-form LP that loses to a basic PDP.

DR Marketing Playbook — Product-Aware Audiences

SECTION 8: QUICK-REFERENCE CHEAT SHEET
Before You Build, Answer These 4 Questions:
18.​Is my audience product-aware? (Yes = this playbook. No = use problem-aware
frameworks.)
19.​What’s their main barrier? (Skepticism = presell with comparison. Complacency = twist
the knife.)
20.​What’s my offer? (If you can’t explain why to buy HERE vs Amazon in 5 seconds, fix
the offer first.)
21.​What third-party page am I running from? (Personal name, community, magazine, or
authority?)

The Golden Rules
•​

Product-aware traffic: Never educate on the problem. Sell the offer and differentiation.

•​

Third-party pages: Match the page name and identity to your avatar. Seed organic
content before running ads.

•​

Presell pages: Almost entirely product comparison. Frame = “we did the homework.”

•​

PDP: Fast, clean, offer-focused. Sticky CTA. CTA above fold. Kill the mechanism
section.

•​

Cross-channel: Retarget Google visitors on Meta with presell. Retarget Meta visitors on
Google with branded search.

•​

Email/SMS: This is where mechanism stories, calculators, and expert quotes belong.
NOT on cold traffic pages.

Diagnose the awareness level before you optimize anything else.
Everything else is secondary.
END OF PLAYBOOK

## SOP: Creating High-Converting Landing Pages for Ads

### Objective

To create effective landing pages for ads that improve customer journey, increase conversion rates, and boost average order value (AOV).

### Key Steps

1. **Understand the Purpose of CRO**: Focus on improving the customer journey to scale effectively.
2. **Choose the Right CRO Strategy**: Opt for ad-focused CRO to test pages and offers specifically for ads.
3. **Use Page Builders**: Utilize tools like Page D\*\*\* for fast and efficient landing page creation.
4. **Design Landing Pages**: Tailor landing pages to match ad angles, avatars, and awareness levels.
5. **Optimize Messaging and Content**: Ensure messaging and content align with ad angles and customer needs.
6. **Test Landing Pages**: Duplicate successful ads and direct traffic to new landing pages for testing.
7. **Analyze Performance**: Monitor conversion rate and average order value to determine success.
8. **Scale Successful Pages**: Scale high-performing landing pages to increase ad spend and revenue.

### Cautionary Notes

- Avoid sending traffic to generic product pages; focus on creating specific landing pages for ads.
- Be cautious of overlapping tests and ensure thorough quality assurance to prevent errors on live Shopify stores.

### Tips for Efficiency

- Utilize page builders like Page Deck for quick and easy landing page creation.
- Focus on improving customer journey and aligning messaging with ad content.
- Test landing pages like you test ads to maximize results.
- Scale successful landing pages quickly to boost ad performance and revenue.

title: "Offer and LP Changes"

author:
published:
created: 2026-06-28
description: "I've previously made the mistake of changing many things on my landing page and offer at once.  Doing it like that didn't isolate any variables so I didn't know"
tags:

Leaderboards

](https://www.skool.com/origins/-/leaderboards)[

About

](https://www.skool.com/origins/about)

1

99+

I've previously made the mistake of changing many things on my landing page and offer at once.

Doing it like that didn't isolate any variables so I didn't know what affected the performance.

How should I go about changing things on my landing page and offer?

Do I literally change one thing at a time?

I takes a while to gather data because of low spend.

Is there a general rule like run it for 7 days?

0

2 comments![Shanil Beekarry](https://assets.skool.com/f/70fd6c0e116d44eab69753e999ee2dff/54aa32683d49497bab85eb9dd307fa4707be73d973094c04837faf66a43e668f-sm.jpg)

Shanil Beekarry[Shanil Beekarry](https://www.skool.com/@shanil-beekarry-4554?g=origins)

• 17h

Yeah, I’d include your A/B testing context like this:

Yeah, I wouldn’t change one tiny thing at a time, but I also wouldn’t change 10 unrelated things at once.

Think in terms of testing one hypothesis at a time.

For example:

“People don’t understand the value fast enough.”

Then you can change a few related things around that: headline, above-the-fold copy, product image, CTA, etc. That’s fine because they all support the same hypothesis.

What you don’t want is changing headline, price, bundle, reviews, offer, shipping, layout, and guarantee all at once, because then you have no idea what caused the result.

For offers, I’d be even more careful. Test offer vs offer clearly through a proper A/B test, because price, bundles, discounts, and guarantees can change buyer behavior a lot.

And I wouldn’t use a fixed “7 day” rule. Run the test until you have enough data, then plug the numbers into an A/B test significance calculator like [abtestguide.com/calc](http://abtestguide.com/calc "http://abtestguide.com/calc") before calling it a winner.

At low spend, focus on bigger changes that can actually move the needle, not tiny CRO tweaks that take forever to validate.

1

• 13h

Sauce, thank you!

Context:

My questions are about how to use primary text and headlines across multiple creatives, whether they should stay the same or be tailored per creative based on the angle, how to come up with strong primary texts and headlines, and how to approach testing creatives on both a standard product page and a separate landing page.

TL;DR:

Primary text & headlines consistency vs tailoring, and how to structure landing page tests.

Question(s):

1. For primary text and headlines: do you initially use the same ones across all three creatives, or do you tailor the primary text and headline per creative based on the angle? (and we’re skipping the description, right?)

2\. How do you come up with strong primary texts and headlines? Is this mainly based on deep research and tools like Claude?

3\. For landing pages: is it a good approach to duplicate the three creatives and test them both on one standard product page and on one separate landing page based on the core avatar (I have a lot of ideas for this)

1. Yes base the primary texts and headlines on the angles, look at competitors for inspiration
2. Look at competitors copy and paste there headlines and primary texts into Claude or ChatGPT, and ask it to analyse what they’ve done to make good ones, then ask it to make 2 of each. Simple as that.
3. Make sure the Landing pages are aligned with your angle and ad, test 1-2 per angle.

2[Sem H](https://www.skool.com/@sem-hulman-4861?g=origins)

1. We always use the top performing one and then a new version. If you don't have a top performing one, then just use two versions.

Yes, we skip the description.

1. Deep research, but also just take inspiration from the copies that are in the winning ads doc, also, somebody created a Google Doc of all of the primary copies from those docs so you can give those to Claude as context.
1. At your level of spend, I wouldn't recommend duplicating ads out like that to test multiple pages just because you will spread your budget too thin. If you create a new page, take your top two to three best ads and then create a new ad set running to it. I covered this in the media buying video, so please watch it.

If you don't know how to code you should remake the landing page in one of the landing page builders that are recommended in the course after claude designs it, or look at landing pages that seems to be working in atria and copy important elements from those. You can't just copy the claude code, you have to tweak it to make it work.

I just launched 5 more landing pages this morning, it takes me about 2 hours to tweak the page how i want it then i just copy and paste them with new angles, they take about 15 minutes per angle after that.

I don't redirect to a product page, they add to cart from my landing page and checkout. Don't want extra clicks for no reason, thats just another reason for a person to think to much and leave before they buy.

Again, i'm no expert i just figured this out on one of our many products. I just launched another product and another set of landing pages to match to see how they work.
